FOREWORD—HISTORICAL AND EXPLANATORY. 
Somn pays after the close of the Summer Quarter of 1908, 
consultation with Alumni members of the Peabody Faculty and 
with Chancellor Porter lent encouragement to a proposed 
scheme for the publication of a complete Alumni Directory of 
Peabody College. The pledge of codperation on the part of 
local alumni interviewed, together with hearty endorsement 
obtained through correspondence with others in different States, 
led to a called meeting of local graduates in August, 1908, in 
order to discuss the feasibility of such an undertaking and to 
deliberate on “ways and means.” This volume is the result of 
that meeting. 
It is not proposed here to give a complete account of the 
working out of plans. The details would make a long story. In 
view of the fact that the work has been somewhat delayed, and 
inasmuch as all of the lists are not entirely complete, it is 
thought not inappropriate, perhaps, to review briefly some of the 
difficulties and labors that have been met with and to indicate 
the degree of persistence that has characterized the efforts to 
make the Directory as nearly complete as possible. . 
As preliminary steps, the College files had to be gone over, 
class lists made out, and historians secured. The magnitude 
of the task that followed, namely, of locating twenty-seven hun- 
dred alumni scattered throughout all parts of the world, is 
better appreciated after the attempt has been made. 
Information, address, and subscription blanks, together with 
an explanatory circular letter, were furnished the Class Histo- 
rians to be enclosed by them in personal letters to the members 
of the different classes. As a result of this first general call, 
hundreds of information blanks, properly filled out, were sent 
in, together with thousands of new addresses (many being dupli- 
cates, of course) and a very encouraging number of subscrip- 
tions to the Directory, a goodly number of which were cash. 
And, as was expected, since the great majority of Peabody grad- 
uates are engaged in an itinerant profession, many letters, in
-P-A-G-E-7
6 ALUMNI DIRBOTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
the course of time, were returned “unclaimed.” In consequence, 
revised class lists were made out and the “delinquents” were 
next appealed to through return postal cards, with fairly good 
results. Then followed, in April, 1909, the distribution of a 
second “circular letter” and a printed “lost” list. Still later, 
August, 1909, a fourth aid final general search was made for the 
unlocated and a last appeal sent to those who had not responded 
from apparent negligence. 
With this statement regarding the scope and magnitude of 
the labor the collection of information has imposed, it must be 
evident that considerable time bas been necessary, especially 
when it is remembered that those in charge of the work had 
other duties demanding much of their time and energy. Many 
alumni have been urging an early publication of the Directory 
at the expense of completeness, suggesting that the delayed 
information that might ultimately be obtained be issued as a 
supplement to the first publication; but it has not been thought 
wise to do this for many evident reasons, but particularly 
because of the additional expense this would entail, and because 
subscribers, it was believed, would prefer as much information 
as possible in one volume, as being more convenient and desir- 
able. Besides, it seemed not well to go to press as long as new 
names and addresses were coming in, and more alumni were all 
the while sending in their belated blanks. 
‘It is indeed to be regretted that the Directory shows so few 
complete lists of classes. To have found all would have been 
beyond expectations, but this did not affect the diligence of the 
search nor lessen the zeal with which every clue has been “run 
down.” Ag an illustration of this persistence, it is recalled that, 
as per the request made, address blanks were returned by 
various alumni giving as many as eight or ten different 
addresses for the same individual, and that letters and sets of 
blanks were forwarded to the various postoffices in turn till 
the person was heard from, or the list exhausted with failure in 
the end. It must be stated in this connection that many names 
and addresses sent in were of very little assistance; for instance, 
those of married alumne without the indicated maiden name. 
These could not be classified, and, in case the communications 
sent direct brought no response, the married name gave no clue. 
But to sum up, the chief cause of delay and incompleteness of
-P-A-G-E-8
HISTORICAL AND EXPLANATORY. q 
this volume has been the failure on the part of the alumni whom 
we reached to send in promptly their own blanks and the names 
of alumni they might have given information about, but neg- 
lected to do so from some cause or other. 
A request: It is to be hoped that alumni who may detect 
errors or omissions in this volume wilJl promptly forward the 
proper addresses and information, in order that corrections 
may be made in the files. The College and the Alumni Asso- 
ciation are very desirous of keeping in close touch with every 
one, and any and all facts that may be given by alumni concern- 
ing themselves or others is most desirable. A card indicating 
any change of address or position, or other information, would 
assist in keeping the files up to date and at the same time 
enable the College to reach the alumni from time to time with 
desirable information and announcements. Peanody College 
has done a great work for the South educationally. Her grad- 
uates have assumed the role of leadership in the Southern States 
in that worthy profession to prepare for which Peabody was 
founded and has been maintained. Working along the same 
lines and with the same ends in view, with experiences and 
ideals in common, Peabody graduates, it would seem, might 
have strong reasons for wanting to know what their comrades 
and friends are doing and accomplishing. Besides, such knowl- 
edge might be mutually helpful. The editor believes a direc- 
tory should be published periodically. With the proper codp- 
eration of all, this can be done. Let the necessary information 
come in. 
Acknowledgments.—The success of the Directory has been 
made possible largely through the codperation and aid of loyal 
friends everywhere. First of all, the good work of the Class’ 
Historians deserves due notice and praise. While other press- 
ing duties prevented some of these from doing all they might - 
have desired, others continued the task diligently to the end, 
some even writing up reports that required very few additions 
or modifications. Alumni are much indebted to Dr. Charles E. 
Little, Prof. J. W. Brister, and Prof. Eugene Tavenner for the 
contributed. articles that appear; and especially to Dr. Little 
and Professor Brister for their excellent advice and able assist- 
ance in so many different ways. Mention should also be made 
of Mr. C. J. Maxwell, class of ’91, of Dallas, Texas, and Miss
-P-A-G-E-9
8 ALUMNI DIREOTORY OF PHABODY COLLEGE. 
Mary D. Pierce, class of 00, of Farmville, Va. Mr. Maxwell 
was for a long time Alumni Historian, and his previously pub- 
lished “Alumni Records” were of much value. Besides, his 
wide acquaintance made it possible for him to render most 
valuable assistance, especially in the location of Texas alumni. 
Miss Pierce is Secretary of the Virginia Peabody Association, 
which position made it possible for her to aid materially in the 
location of Virginia alumni. To all who have contributed in 
any way to the success of this Directory, the Editor takes this 
opportunity to express his profound gratitude. 
"Very respectfully and fraternally, 
I. S. Wamrrer, 
President Alumni Association and Editor Alumni Directory. 
Naswvinix, TENn., December 14, 1909.

  EBEN SPERRY STEARNS. 
BORN DECEMBER 23, 1819; DIED APRIL 11, 1887. 
First President of the College, 1875-1887, who did pioneer service in gaining 
recognition for the value of professionally trained teachers, and who lived to see the 
permanency and worth of the College acknowledged by all the South.
-P-A-G-E-60
BIOGRAPHICAL. 
Biographical Sketches’ of Peabody Graduates by Classes; Giving . Original and Present Addresses of Alumni; Years in Attendance 
at Peabody; Degrees, and Dates of Same; Positions held since Graduation; Etc.; Etc. 
EXPLANATION, 
Alumni having completed courses above the L. I. Diploma, are listed in the year of their ranking College Degree, the rule of precedence being arbitrarily established as fol- lows: A. B., B.S.,B.L., BP. AL M., M.S. 
The letter “O” in parenthesis, thus (0), indicates original home address; the letter “Pp”, present home address; the letter “B”, present business address; the letter “8”, stu- dent at Peabody College, and the letter “M”, married.

P. S—The foregoing records are incomplete, owing to the 
inability of the Historian to correspond with many who might 
have given clues. The private letters from classmates were 
nearly all bright with hope and good cheer, all loyal to the old 
Peabody. We hope to locate the missing and have a class 
reunion in a year or two. Let us all help this to come to pass. 
T. J. Woorrer.

early age, Miss Moore was sent to school by her grandfather,

Notr.—On account of the increasing difficulty as the years 
go by in getting full and accurate information concerning the 
deceased alumni, your Historian has taken special pains to try 
to get a full report of each deceased alumnus, but regrets to say 
that in several instances he has been unable to get in touch with 
any members of the family, or any friends of the deceased who 
7
-P-A-G-E-103
98 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
could give any accurate data concerning them, and in other 
instances the parties failed to reply. The report herewith sub- 
mitted is as full on essentials as your Historian could make it. 
D. SayLor Goon.

  WILLIAM HAROLD PAYNE. 
BORN MAY 12, 1836; DIED JUNE 18, 1907. 
President of the College from 1887 to 1901, the period of its greatest expansion, 
He inspired thousands of his students, who caught his enthusiasm for education: 
“Teaching is the noblest of professions, but the sorriest of trades.”

CHAFFIN, HOWARD WM.—(0) Kokomo, Ind.; (P) 111 W. 
Markland Ave., Kokomo, Ind.; (S) 79498; taught until 
°01, since which date has served as letter carrier in Kokomo, 
Ind.; °96’08; worked toward his B. 8. Degree; near the 
close of the session of the year ’07—08, at which time he 
was expecting to graduate, somé differences arose between 
him and the administration of the College, and he was dis- 
missed. Subsequent investigations seemed to indicate that 
an injustice had been done Mr. Chaffin, but the matter was 
never taken up until attention was called to it by some 
correspondence incident to the collection of facts for the 
“Directory.” In Aug., 09, Governor Porter, Chancellor of 
the Univ. Nashville, authorized his reinstatement, thus 
removing the stigma of dismissal.

BORN DECEMBER 7, 1828. 
President of the College from 1901 to 1909, resigning in the summer of the latter 
year. Under his administration the College was developed in lines distinctly 
broader and higher than the Normal School, and also the plan for Greater Peahody 
was grandly conceived and carried forward.

*The school year extends from June to June, the Summer Session (first 
organized in 1902), being regarded as the first quarter of the school year. 
For instance, students and graduates of the summer of ’02 are counted in 
their proper places in the session of ’02-’03, those of the summer of ’09, in 
the present session ’09-’10, ete. 
{Some of the graduates counted in ’02-’03 and ’03-’04 finished their 
work the preceding summers. No diplomas were granted at the summer 
quarter prior to '04. 
}The large enrollment for the summer of ’02 was partly due to the fact 
that no tuition fees were charged that summer, and partly because of the 
special arrangements made with the Davidson County Teachers who were 
registered for two of the eight weeks. 
The above table, together with the following graphs should be studied 
in connection with the table at the end of the article on Standards, page 364.

-P-A-G-E-351
  ROBERT CHARLES WINTHROP. 
BORN MAY 12, 1809; DIED NOVEMBER 16, 1894. 
The close friend of George Peabody; first Chairman of the Peabody Board, 
1867-1894. He was active in founding Peabody College; and his last words, in a 
letter to his colleagues just before his death, were an earnest appeal to endow “our 
great Normal College at Nashville” with at least one million dollars.

STANDARD OF PEABODY COLLEGE FOR 
TEACHERS, 1875-1909. 
By Dr. Caartes E. Lirrin, Proressor or Latin. 
Tue College has varied in name as well as in standard during 
the course of-its development, each showing a’distinct evolution. 
It was known indifferently from 1875 to 1889 as the State 
Normal School, or the State Normal College. In 1889 it was 
officially designated as the Peabody Normal College, and in 
1903 as the Peabody College for Teachers. It has never been 
a chartered institution, but has used as far as necessary the 
chartered rights of the corporation known since 1825 as the 
University of Nashville. 
The age at which students were admitted was, from 1875 to 
1892, fixed by a minimum and a maximum—16 to 30 years; 
but the upper limit was removed in 1892. Scholarship students 
were, from 1876 to 1880, under the same restrictions as to age; 
but from 1880 to 1892 the limits were 17 to 30. From 1892 to 
1904 the upper limit was sometimes stated for scholarship stu- 
dents, but was practically not in force. 
In the statement of standards it will be necessary to make 
comparisons by measuring courses of study in terms of the 
Carnegie system, now so generally understood and adopted. 
Students entering the’ first or Junior class were required to 
show preparation in the grammar-school subjects; but a fuller 
education was recommended, as the publications of the College 
distinctly state, that it “aims to be professional and will review 
rapidly the subjects to be taught and give instruction mainly 
in methods and in higher branches.” 
Students holding scholarships were required to show evi- 
dence of a high-school education. The term, “high school,” was 
loosely used, and at its lowest might mean the first year of high 
school (the ninth grade). 
23 (353)
-P-A-G-E-369
354 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PHABODY COLLEGE. 
The curriculum as first announced, in 1875, and as kept in 
force for several years is as follows: 
Junior Class (first year): First term—grammar school 
studies with reference to teaching them; second term—geom- 
etry, chemistry, United States history, physiology and hygiene, 
map drawing, etc.; third term—geometry, chemistry, rhetoric, 
book-keeping. : 
Middle Class (second year): First term—algebra, English 
literature, natural history, natural philosophy; second term— 
trigonometry, intellectual philosophy, geology, English litera- 
ture; third term—general history, trigonometry, botany, intel- 
lectual philosophy. . 
Senior Class (third year) : First term—calculus, astronomy, 
United States constitution, school laws of Tennessee, moral 
science, practice in model school ; second term—calculus, astron- 
omy, reviews in elementary and high-school studies, English 
elassics, practice; third term—physics and natural history 
with reference to object teaching, theory and art of teaching, 
practice, languages, etc. reviewed. 
Latin, French, and German are announced to be taught in 
“a parallel course,” but doubtless were not given at all: the 
faculty of those early years was very small, and the students 
were mainly preparing for work in the grades and not in high 
schools. 
In 1879 a restatement of the curriculum is made, including 
practically the same subjects; though additional studies now 
offered are zodlogy in the Middle class and Latin in the Middle 
and Senior classes, while growth at the upper end is shown by 
the establishment of a fourth or Baccalaureate class. Note the 
changed names: natural philosophy has become physics, intel- 
lectual philosophy has become psychology. The curriculum 
follows: 
Junior Class (first year)—primary and grammar-school 
studies with reference to teaching: Arithmetic, algebra, geog- 
raphy, English grammar, United States history, physiology and 
hygiene, rhetoric and etymology, book-keeping, freehand 
drawing. 
Middle Class (second year)—-geometry, plane trigonometry, 
geology, zodlogy, botany, general history, moral science, Latin, 
perspective drawing.
-P-A-G-E-370
STANDARD OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 355 
Senior Class (third year)—-spherical geometry and trigo- 
nometry, astronomy, chemistry, physics, English literature, 
United States constitution, Latin, psychology. 
Baccalaureate Class (fourth year)—conic sections, analytic 
geometry, calculus, English literature, Latin, German, psychol- 
ogy, drawing. 
From this date on, the system of scholarships operated to 
make the standard higher than the attainments required would 
indicate; for competitive examinations proved the means of 
selecting a fine body of young persons who proposed to teach. 
At the same time this system tended to eliminate the first or 
Junior class from the actual curriculum, making it virtually a 
preparatory year; for scholarship holders were required (1880 
on) to be 17 years old and prepared to enter the Middle 
class. Thus the majority of the mature and well-prepared 
students, theoretically spent two years to reach the L. I. 
diploma, and three years for the A. B. degree. And most of © 
them actually did so, in spite of the fact that many came from 
high schools of creditable standard and that to complete the 
course for L. I. meant hardly more scholarship than that given 
in the first three years of a present-day four-year high school; 
while the A. B. degree fitted one for admission little, if any, 
above the Freshman class of a standard college of today. But 
the college standard was low then all over the South, and the 
high school corresponded generally to the lowest colleges. 
The experiment with the Baccalaureate class did not succeed 
at this time, and the A. B. degree was discontinued in 1883: the 
tone of the College had been set for the next ten years by the 
two-year course for L. I. and by the two-year tenure of scholar- 
ships. 
Some outward events show a grasp of the definite type of 
work to be done and better means for doing it: lectures on eivil 
government and pedagogics were begun in 1883, a chapel was 
fitted up the same year, a gymnasium in 1884, 
This was the status of the College to the end of President 
Stearns’s administration in the spring of 1887. When Pres- 
ident Payne entered office in the fall of that year, little imme- 
diate change was made. But 1888 and the next year are pivotal 
in the history and growth of the College. In 1888 the school 
year was divided into two terms instead of three, and called
-P-A-G-E-371
356 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
semesters in 1889; a more careful statement of content for each 
course and subject was made; the Baccalaureate class was 
added again, and in 1889 post-graduate work for A. M.; in 1889 
the name was changed to Peabody Normal College and the 
entire class system altered—Junior became Freshman, Middle 
became Sophomore, Senior became Junior, and Baccalaureate 
became Senior. In the same year, the whole spirit and outlook ~ 
of the College was altered: scholarship became of even greater 
moment than professional subjects, and enthusiasm for high 
fitness in a noble calling prevailed. French, German, and 
Greek are henceforth offered along with Latin. For the first 
time in the history of the College something like an adequate 
faculty was gathered and the young men had some collective life 
in thirty-odd rooms in Lindsley Hall (thrown open in 1888), 
while the library facilities were greatly enlarged and all books 
moved to Lindsley Hall. College spirit and college ideals grew 
apace with the sudden increase in number of students, number 
of instructors, number of courses and subjects and items of 
equipment. The College has felt no such sudden expansion since 
that time. Its growth as a vital force in Southern education 
was phenomenal for some ten years, since which time the process 
of growth has been internal, without proper readjustment to 
present currents of educational thought. Its movement has 
been latterly toward realizing the ideals of scholarship and 
standard set before itself in the formative, enthusiastic years 
1888 to 1898. Despite its recent growth in the matter of stand- 
ard, the College has swerved more and.more into an eddy of 
isolation. Circumstances well known to the public have pre- 
vented an aggressive outward policy, and that need is now the 
most powerful argument for turning loose this pent-up energy: 
the College needs again to attach itself to the live issues of 
Southern education, and to lead them as it once did. 
The changes of 1889 were important. While there was no 
formal elevation of standard, either for admission or gradua- 
tion, there was a powerful stimulus derived from many factors. 
‘Real life and vigor were put into every department of work. 
Review subjects were eliminated; to enter the Freshman (old 
Junior) class required the equivalent of the grammar-school 
subjects, and most students entered Sophomore (old Middle) 
class, taking L. I. in two years and A. B. in three. Academic
-P-A-G-E-372
* STANDARD OF PEABODY COLLEGE. . 357 
work was sound, professional work was illuminating. There 
was a small margin of electives for personal choice. Some 
sequence and grouping of courses was put in force. The details 
of the curricula follow: 
1. L. I. Courssz, 1889, 
There were prescribed: Latin 4 courses, two years (for 
those without preparation—beginner’s Latin, Caesar, Cicero; 
for those with a year’s preparation—Cesar, Cicero, Sallust, 
Livy; for those with two or three years’ preparation—Sallust, 
Livy, Cicero’s Brutus, Ovid) ; psychology 1 course; pedagogy 
4 courses; mathematics 6 courses (arithmetic, algebra; plane 
geometry, plane trigonometry) ; English 6 courses (rhetoric, 
American literature, history of literature); history 5 courses 
(American, English, geography) ; chemistry 1 course; physics 
1 course; music 4 courses. 
Most of these courses were three hours a week, some two 
hours; a full course was theoretically five hours a week for half 
ayear., The above sums up sixteen and three-fifths full courses, 
leaving elective one and two-fifths full courses. The require- 
ment for the L. I. diploma was eighteen full courses: 18X5=—90 
hours. With fifteen hours a week, a student did thirty hours 
a year, or the ninety hours in three years. : 
2. A. B. Courss, 1889, 
There were prescribed the sixteen and three-fifths ‘full 
courses as required for L. I., and in addition: Latin 2 courses 
(Horace, Quintilian) ; Greek 4 courses; pedagogy 2 courses; 
French or German 2 courses; English 1 course; biology 1 
course. This sums up twenty-three and one-fifth full courses, 
leaving three-fifths of a course elective. The requirement for 
the A. B. degree was twenty-four full courses: 24 X 5=120 
hours, or a course of four years. 
3. B. 8. Course, 1889. 
There were prescribed no Greek, more science, but otherwise 
the subjects were along the same lines as for A. B.; the sum, 
however, was twenty-four full courses, with two elective. The
-P-A-G-E-373
858 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
requirement for the B. S. degree was twenty-six full courses: 
26 X 5= 180 hours of work for the four years. 
4. B. L. Courss, 1889. 
There were prescribed no Latin or Greek, more French or 
German, and more English; the sum “was twenty-one full 
courses, leaving five elective. The requirement for the B. L. 
degree was twenty-six full courses: 26 X 5= 130 hours of work 
for the four years. 
In 1892 these three degrees were all put on the same level, 
with twenty-five full courses required for each: 25 X 5=125 
hours of work for the four years. 
5. Grapuatn Work, 1889. 
One year of graduate work was required in three lines (a 
major and two minors) to receive the A. M. degree. 
Work in absentia as well as in residence was at first allowed, 
but work in absentia was discontinued in 1893. When the 
standard of the College was raised in 1903 graduate work was 
no longer allowed. 
In all this reform by President Payne (and the success of 
it was marvelous), there was no real advance in formal stand- ~ 
ard; but as already shown, the College was above the plane of 
- its own claims. Students could still enter the Freshman (old 
Junior) class with a grammar-school education and graduate 
with an A. B., B. S., or B. L. degree in four years. These 
degrees were, therefore, when formally and mechanically esti- 
mated, about equivalent to a present-day high-school diploma; 
but as a matter of fact, most of the students were graduates of 
fairly good high schools and entered the Sophomore or the 
Junior class. A fairer estimate would rate these degrees as 
sufficient to admit the strongest among their holders to the 
Sophomore class of a standard college. * 
In 1891 the entrance requirements were raised in mathe- 
matics so as to include arithmetic and algebra to quadratics, 
and students were advised (not required) to offer a year’s 
preparation in Latin. Here we find the College for the first 
time stating its definition of a high-school course for admission. 
These requirements had generally been more than met in the
-P-A-G-E-374
STANDARD OF PEABODY COLLHGE. 359 
earlier days of the College, but increased numbers applying for 
admission made definition and restriction necessary. But by 
formal measurement the above requirements of genuine high- 
school value would count as 1 Carnegie unit in algebra and 1 
unit in Latin—a total of 2 units as compared with.14 units now 
required. : 
In 1898 another advance was made in admission require- 
ments: arithmetic and algebra equivalent to 1 unit; English 
grammar 1 unit; geography and United States history 1 or 
11% units—a total of 3 of 4 units. . 
In 1894 a considerable raise was made possible by the great 
number seeking admission: English (grammar, composition, 
rhetoric, at least two classics) equivalent to about 2 Carnegie 
units; geography and United States history, 1 or 14% units; 
mathematics (arithmetic, algebra to quadratics, plane geometry 
two books) 11% units; Latin 1 unit—a total of 51% or 6 units, 
which must in fairness be concretely reckoned as two years of 
the high school. 
In this same year an important further differentiation 
between the L. I. diploma and the College degrees was made. 
For L. I. and for the degrees the same admission subjects (514 ° 
or 6 Carnegie units) were required; but the curriculum leading 
to L. I. was for two years or 69 hours; leading to A. B. was for 
four years or 135 hours (10 hours elective); leading to B. S. 
and to B. L. was for four years or 140 hours (17 hours elective 
for the former and 12 for the latter). This put the L. I. work 
on the Normal School basis and the degree work on a Junior 
College (Freshman and Sophomore) basis at the very least. 
The conerete facts warrant us here also in rating each of them 
somewhat higher. 
President Payne tried two experiments at expansion during 
this period, both of which failed. He established a degree in 
art and literature called Bachelor of Painting (B. P.), which, 
though offered from 1893 to 1900, counts only four graduates. 
He also established a College of Music by an alliance with a 
Nashville conservatory; but the union was short-lived (1895— 
97), and marked a distinct danger of retrogression in the stand- 
ard of the College. 
From 1894 for about seven years, no additional requirements 
for admission were made nor changes in the curriculum; but in
-P-A-G-E-375
360 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGH. 
1901 the requirements were slightly advanced in English and 
in mathematics, the whole being as follows: English (grammar, 
composition, rhetoric, several classics) 214 or 3 units; geogra- 
phy and United States history 1 or 1144 units; mathematics 
(arithmetic, algebra through quadratics, plane geometry three 
books) 2 units; Latin 1 unit—a total of about 7 units or fully 
(and perhaps even more than) the usual first two years of high 
school. 
The administration of President Porter covered the eight 
years from 1901 to 1909, in which was accomplished the move. 
ment begun by President Payne in the matter of reaching a full 
college standard. Jn 1903 a difference was made for the first 
time between the requirements for admission to the L. I. course 
and to the A. B. course. All the degrees previously offered 
(A. B., B. 8., B. L.) were given the common name, A. B.; but 
opportunity to make even more pronounced differences than 
formerly was provided for by mainly prescribing the work for 
Freshman and Sophomore and by leaving it mainly elective for 
Junior and Senior. The admission requirements were stated 
in units, but with values not at all like the present Carnegie 
units. The values for the subjects as given in units by the 
College for admission to the Freshman class are put in paren- 
theses, followed by the value in Carnegie units: English (4 
units) 3 Carnegie units—college entrance requirements; math- 
ematics (4) 2 Carnegie units—arithmetic, algebra through 
quadratics, plane geometry three books; Latin (3) 3 Carnegie 
units—beginner’s Latin, Cesar, Cicero; United States history 
and geography (2) 1 Carnegie unit; making (13) 9 Carnegie 
units prescribed, with (2) 2 Carnegie units elective (to be 
chosen from Greek, Gernian, mathematics, physics, history). 
The total requirements were, therefore, (15) 11 Carnegie units 
or about a three-year high-school course. 
When this standard was established in 1903, it represented 
the general level of the best Southern institutions—e. g., the 
great majority of those in the Association of Colleges and Pre- 
paratory Schools of the Southern States. 
The requirements for admission to the L. I. course was very 
slightly changed from the 1894 and the 1901 standard: English 
(4, same as for A. B. course) 3.Carnegie units; Mathematics 
(4, same'as for A. B. course) 2 Carnegie units; Latin (1) 1
-P-A-G-E-376
STANDARD OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 361 
Carnegie unit; United States history and geography (2) 1 
Carnegie unit; making a total of (11) 7 Carnegie units. The 
course leading to the L. I. diploma was for two years, and was 
practically all prescribed. 
The Summer Quarter was established in 1902 and ran for 
six weeks; from 1903 it has been run during eight weeks. The 
attendance at the first Summer Quarter was 459, the largest 
in its history—due to the fact that no fee was charged and that 
the Davidson County teachers held their institute and regis- 
tered in the College for two weeks. The fee was $5 from 1903 
to 1908, and $10 in 1909. The attendance has grown steadily, 
and the work has formed a powerful adjunct to the regular 
session of three quarters. In the summer of 1903 there were 
151 students; in 1904, 174; im 1905, 288; in 1906, 284; in 
1907, 310; in 1908, 376; and in 1909, 305. 
President Porter further signalized his policy by taking in 
1908 the last step to make Peabody College equal in standard to 
the best. As in 1903 it had been raised to college rank when 
measured by the Southern standard, so in 1908 it was raised to 
college rank when measured by the national standard for col- 
leges—fourteen admission units, followed by a four-year course; 
as illustrated concretely by Harvard, Yale, Princeton, Columbia, 
ete., and as formulated in 1906 by the Carnegie Foundation for 
the Advancement of Teaching. 
Aside from raising the standard to fourteen Carnegie units 
for admission to the Freshman class the College was completely 
reorganized in 1908: The College (A. B. course) was separated 
from the Normal School (L. I. course) by standard and by 
type of courses, the students were taught in different classes, 
the tasks imposed varied in accordance with these differences 
of standard; the college year was extended from 32 to 36 weeks, 
divided into three quarters of twelve weeks each, and a summer 
quarter of eight weeks; for admission to the College 14 units 
were required, to the Normal School 814 units; each course in 
the College called for five recitations a week, three courses to 
be the average amount taken simultaneously by a student; in 
the Normal School each course called for four recitations a 
week, four courses to be taken at a time; the sequence of 
courses and choice of those prescribed was wrought out upon a 
scientific principle; in the College, Freshman and Sophomore
-P-A-G-E-377
362 ALUMNI DIREOTORY OF PHABODY COLLEGE. 
were mainly prescribed, Junior and Senior were mainly elective; 
in the Normal Schoo] the courses were mainly prescribed for 
both years. Some of the details follow: 
1. Tux Corizex, 1908, 
Fourteen units were required for admission to the Freshman 
class, divided into three groups: 
Group I—English 3 units, mathematics 3, Latin 4, Greek 3, 
Greek and Roman history 1—a total of 14 units. This group 
~ corresponds to the old A. B. course of the classical colleges. 
Group IIi—Prescribed: English 3 units, mathematics 3, 
Latin 4, physics 1—making 11 units; elective: foreign language 
2 units or 1, history 1 unit or 2—making 3 units; prescribed 
and elective together making a total of 14 units. This group 
corresponds to the old Latin-scientific course, which led usually 
to the B. 8. degree. 
Group IlI—Prescribed: English 3 units, mathematics 3, 
physics 1—making 7 units; elective: history 2 units, foreign 
language 4, science 1—making 7 units; prescribed and elective 
together making a total of 14 units. This group corresponds 
to the scientific and the modern language courses, which usually 
led to the B. S. and B. L. degree respectively. 
The College curriculum was divided into three correspond- 
ing groups, leading from these admission groups and built defi- 
nitely upon them. It is believed that the suggestions put into 
this course in 1908 and now so successfully in operation will 
receive the hearty indorsement of all enlightened educators, 
when the work of Peabody College comes to be recognized at its 
full worth. 
All three of these groups start with prescribed work in lan- 
guages, mathematics, and inorganic sciences; they then con- 
tinue with literature, history, and biologic sciences; and close 
the prescribed list with philosophy and anthropologic sciences. 
The elective courses of Junior and Senior years are built upon 
this sequence, which has not merely a high fitness as the sub- 
structure of any college course, but is well adapted to form an 
almost ideal introduction to the study of human education— 
the special province of a college for teachers.
-P-A-G-E-378
STANDARD OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 363 
2. Tus.Normat ScHoon, 1908. 
For admission to the L. I. course there were required: 
" English 3 units, mathematics 214 units (raised 44 unit in plane 
geometry), Latin or French or German 2 units (raised 1 unit), 
history and geography 1 unit—a total of 81% units. While the 
entrance requirements were but slightly raised, the sequence of 
studies was entirely changed and the two-year course made far 
more scientific and better adapted to training elementary 
teachers. 
An attempt is made to summarize the preceding statements 
into one general view by the table given below. The standard 
admission requirements are represented by the four years of the 
high school (built upon the eight elementary grades), and the 
standard college course is likewise represented by four years 
(Freshman, Sophomore, Junior, Senior) built upon the high 
school. The horizontal lines in the diagram represent the 
amount both of admission requirements and of college work 
covered by Peabody at the dates indicated. True, this is but a 
mechanical and crude way of illustrating values, and cannot 
give the spirit and potentiality of real life. The dotted part of 
the horizontal lines represents the admission units and the 
solid part represents the four-year course of the College.

LITERARY SOCEITIES AND SOCIAL CLUBS. 
By Eveenn Tavenner, Cuass or 799, Assistant PRorrssor 
or Latin. 
Tr it be true that the inner life and spirit of a body of college 
students can often be more accurately judged from their volun- 
tarily organized activities than from their more or less com- 
pulsory class-room work, surely a volume devoted to and 
intended for the alumni of any institution would be an incom- 
plete representation of life as it was and is today at the alma 
mater, without a chapter concerning the literary and social 
activities of students past and present. And this is even more 
true when the organizations under consideration have so honor- 
able and long continued a past history as have those of Peabody 
College for Teachers, and its literary predecessor, the University 
of Nashville. 
Indeed, two of the literary societies, the Agatheridan and the 
Erosophian, had celebrated their first birthday when the Uni- 
versity of Nashville was officially organized under its present 
name (1826); nor have they to this day ceased to feel if not to 
exercise the rights of primogeniture. 
Born at the same time, reared under the same influences, they 
have grown in similar strength, their sinews waxing strong 
from friendly and constant struggles for mastery in oratory 
and debate, until now they have come to be the literary guard- 
ians of successive bands of students, and have been both model 
and inspiration for numerous literary societies or more recent 
origin. 
To the good fortune of both societies, victory in their various 
contests has been throughout the years evenly divided, though 
members of both will question the accuracy of this statement. 
Bqual honor, too, has come to them in the matter of distin- 
guished membership in the past; the Agatheridans counting 
(365)
-P-A-G-E-381
366 ALUMNI DIREHOTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
among their former presidents Gov. James D. Porter, but 
recently retired from the presidency of Peabody College for . 
Teachers, and Hon. J. M. Dickinson, present Secretary of War 
for the United States; while the Erosophians number among 
their members Sam Davis, the Southern hero, and a majority of 
the alumni members of the present faculty. 
With the establishment of Peabody College for Teachers 
upon the grounds of the University of Nashville, in 1875, and 
the advent of a mych augmented student body, the two vener- 
able rivals for literary honors found not only that their own 
numbers were increasing in a very gratifying manner, but that 
anew society had sprung into existence, in order to take care of 
the overflow. 
This was the Adelphi Society, an organization that main- 
tained a vigorous existence during the last decade of the nine- 
teenth and the first few years of the present century. Notwith- 
standing its youth, the new society produced some very able 
debaters and orators, whose efforts more than once snatched vic- 
tory from the representatives of the older societies. After a 
worthy life of a dozen years, the Adelphi society was voluntarily 
abandoned on account of the decreased number of men students 
in college, leaving the Agatheridan and Erosophian societies, as 
of old, to hold high the light of oratory and present annually 
their rival claims for supremacy. Both of these societies have 
continued and are now in a flourishing condition. 
With the increase in the number of women students at Pea- 
body, as at all Colleges for Teachers, there came in rapid suc- 
cession the organization of the Girls’ Chapter, the Peabody 
Literary Society, and the Alpha Phi Literary Society, in 1880, 
1889, and 1895, respectively. 
These organizations among the young ladies have had as 
their aim the same general purpose as did the men’s societies, 
with the usual feminine additions in the way of social evenings, 
at homes, musical programs, and other activities of a similar 
sort; on which occasions the social element is supreme, and 
hours are spent that will in after years prove perhaps to be the 
most precious recollections of all the college days. 
They have also gone more into the field of topical studies 
after the manner of women’s clubs, and have found it a very 
instructive and pleasing field of activity. In these societies, as
-P-A-G-E-382
LITERARY SOCIETIES AND SOCIAL CLUBS. 367 
in their masculine prototypes, the spirit of rivalry has been a 
constant spur to increased activity, and the inter-society con- 
tests in oratory and debate have been an interesting feature of 
college life, as well as a distinct training for the participants. 
For many years the young ladies’ literary sacieties had no 
permanent homes, but within recent years each of them has been 
given adequate and comfortable quarters upon the campus— 
each a center where congenial spirits may meet and rest and 
chat throughout the week as well as conduct their programs. 
A great profusion of college pennants, a couch strewn with 
embroidered cushions, a piano, pictures, a punch bowl with 
attendant glasses, and groups of members, each interested in 
some particularly absorbing topic—more or less secret, of 
course—these are the physical surroundings of a girls’ society 
existence. And who will say that her college life is not broad- 
ened and her spirit refreshed by just this relief from the too fre- 
quently grinding effort of the class room? 
Thus far we have spoken of those societies only that were 
literary in object and were social centers incidentally. We have 
yet to mention a group of organizations of the past, which 
sought their members not from any prospect of literary power, 
but on account of congeniality of spirit; clubs that had refined 
social enjoyment as their chief if not their only aim. These 
were, in the order of their establishment, the Bric-a-brac, the 
Salmagundi, the Non Pareil, and the Lotus Club. Their exist- 
ence was brilliant though short-lived. ; 
They were all limited in membership, the usual number being 
apparently twelve young gentlemen and the same number of 
young ladies. They flourished from about 1880 to 1892, when 
the growth of the Student Christian Association movement, 
together with its social features, seems gradually to have 
crowded these clubs out of existence. 
Their nearest likeness at the present time, in close congenial- 
ity without any effort toward literary achievement, is perhaps 
found in the sororities at Peabody—Sigma Sigma Sigma and 
Delta Theta Beta. The latter of these seems to be a local soror- 
ity, while the former has chapters in many of the Southern 
States. The Delta Theta Beta sorority was organized in 1903, 
and the Sigma Sigma Sigma one year later. Both sororities 
hold regular meetings throughout the college year in their
-P-A-G-E-383
368 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
respective places of assembly, and each is the center of a healthy 
struggle for college honors. 
There are, then, in existence at Peabody, two literary socie- 
ties for men, three similar organizations with slightly different 
purposes for women, and two sororities, each a source of social 
enjoyment as well as an inspiration to literary activity. In 
class and college politics—and class lines are now more rigidly 
drawn and class loyalty much more generally cultivated than 
formerly—these organizations frequently wield a deciding influ- 
ence, as they do in the management of the college journal. 
Each organization is eagerly awaiting the time when the 
expansion of the college will mean for them a glorious increase 
in membership, a renewed joy of more vigorous existence, and 
the buoyancy of a hopeful future. For this larger existence 
they are well prepared, and to this larger institution they will 
impart not the least of its glories.

SKETCH OF THE FINANCIAL HISTORY OF 
PEABODY COLLEGE FOR TEACHERS. 
By J. W. Brisrer, Prormssor or MaTHEMATICS. 
Tue history of. Peabody College for Teachers is almost 
eoéxtensive with that of the Trustees of the Peabody Education 
Fund. 
On October 3, 1866, George Peabody, in consultation with’ 
Robert C. Winthrop, made known his purpose to do something 
for the cause of education in those sections of the country which 
had suffered most during the great civil struggle but recently 
closed. 
The donation was formally made by Mr. Peabody, February 
7, 1867, in a letter addressed to the following gentlemen: Hon. 
Robert C. Winthrop, Massachusetts; Hon. Hamilton Fish, New 
York; Right Rev. Chas. P. McIlvaine, Ohio; Gen. U. S. Grant, 
of the United States Army; Hon. William C. Rives, Virginia; 
Hon. John H. Clifford, Massachusetts; Hon. William Aiken, 
South Carolina; William M. Evarts, Esq., New York; Hon. 
William A. Graham, North Carolina; Charles Macalester, Esq:, 
Pennsylvania; George W. Riggs, Esq., Washington, D. C.; 
Samuel Wetmore, Esq., New York; Edward A. Bradford, Esq., 
Louisiana; George N. Eaton, Esq., Maryland; and George Pea- 
body Russell, Esq., Massachusetts. 
In this letter he referred to the “educational needs of those 
portions of our beloved and ‘common country which: have 
suffered from the destructive ravages, and the not less disastrous 
consequences, of Civil War.” And he placed at the disposal of 
the gentlemen addressed, the sum. of one million dollars; “for 
the promotion and encouragement of intellectual, moral, or 
industrial education among the young of the more destitute 
portions of the Southern and Southwestern States.” |. 
In addition to this gift of a million dollars, the letter also’ 
24 (369)
-P-A-G-E-387
~ 
870 ALUMNI DIRHCTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
transmitted to the trustees bonds of the State of Mississippi, 
amounting to eleven hundred thousand dollars. The gentlemen 
. named above received this letter and these gifts, at a meeting 
held in Washington on February 8, 1867, and proceeded at once 
to the organization of the Trustees of the Peabody Education 
Fund. 
In a subsequent letter to the Board, Mr. Peabody enlarged 
his gift by a million dollars, together with $384,000.00 of bonds 
of the Territory of Florida. 
The Peabody Board, from the very outset, put itself on 
record as favoring the training of teachers. At its meeting in 
March, 1867, it declared for the promotion of “Common School 
Education ;” and to this end, expressed its purpose to further 
“Normal School Education” by the endowment of scholarships 
in existing Southern institutions, by establishing normal 
schools, and by aiding any normal school then in operation in 
the Southern and Southwestern States. 
At this same meeting Barnas Sears, then President of Brown 
University, was elected General Agent of the Board. He at 
once entered upon a systematic campaign for advancing the 
cause of education in the territory which the Peabody Board 
- was organized to aid. He heartily concurred in the expressed 
plan of the Board to assist in the training of teachers, and 
wherever he went he strenuously urged the establishment of 
normal schools. 
In November, 1867, Dr. Sears visited Tennessee. At Nash- 
ville he conferred with members of the legislature, leading 
educators, and State authorities; and plans were discussed 
for the early establishment of normal schools to which he prom- 
ised generous assistance. 
-Dr. Sears never abandoned this idea, and finally, in 1875, 
arrangements were completed for the opening of the Normal 
College at Nashville. He was especially anxious to secure a 
legislative appropriation, but the revenues of the State were not 
in condition at that time to warrant it. But at the suggestion 
of Hon. James D. Porter, then Governor of the State, and a 
member of the Board of Trustees of the University of Nashville, 
the Jatter generously donated their campus and buildings for 
the school, as well as the interest on certain funds in their pos- 
session, amounting to $3,000.00 per annum. The College was °
-P-A-G-E-388
FINANCIAL HISTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 371 
legally established March 3, 1875, and was formally opened with 
appropriate exercises, December 1, 1875, with Dr. E. S. Stearns 
as President. 
The authorities of Tennessee had promised State aid as soon 
as conditions would warrant, but the treasury of Tennessee was 
practically depleted, and for several years no appropriation was 
made. This failure on the part of the State led Dr. Sears to 
seriously consider the question of the removal of the College 
from Nashville. In his report to the Peabody Board, October, 
1879, he called attention to the fact that Montgomery Bell 
Academy was occupying part of the College building, that the 
College had already outgrown its narrow accommodations, and 
that its numbers were rapidly increasing. He spoke of his 
having called the attention of the State and University author- 
ities to these conditions, with no relief, while the inaction of 
the legislature was deeply deplored. 
The Peabody Board recognized the gravity of the situation, 
as depicted by Dr. Sears, and requested him to report at a later 
meeting whether it would be advisable to cease relations with 
the Nashville school and make arrangements with some other 
Southern institution. To this end Dr. Sears and Dr. Stearns 
visited Georgia in the spring of 1880 to consider propositions 
from certain institutions. During the visit Dr. Sears con- 
tracted a severe cold, from which he never recovered, and in 
consequence made no report of the negotiations. 
But Dr. Stearns, in an elaborate report to the Peabody 
Board in December, 1880, set forth all the details. The Georgia 
Legislature agreed to make an adequate annual appropriation 
for the Normal School, but with such restrictions as to find 
little favor with Dr. Sears. Two places were making bids for 
the institution—Atlanta and Athens; and of the two, Atlanta 
was most favorably considered. But, Dr. Stearns says in his 
report, in spite of what was then regarded as a liberal proposi- 
tion, there were seemingly insurmountable difficulties in the 
wny of its acceptance; and both he and Dr. Sears left Atlanta 
with the hope that terms could be made with the authorities at 
Nashville which would insure the permanent location of the 
school here. On Dr. Stearns’s return to Nashville he set before 
the Trustees of the University of Nashville the conditions neces- 
sary to retain the College. They were unable to agree to these,
-P-A-G-E-389
872 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGH. 
aud the friends of the College in Nashville regarded its removal 
to Atlanta as inevitable. Dr. Stearns actually wired to Dr. 
Sears that he agreed to the removal if he (Sears) thought best, 
and the matter seemed settled. But a day or two later nego- 
tiations were redpened, and at a massmeeting of prominent citi- 
zens of Nashville, the necessary funds were guaranteed and all 
other conditions agreed to, These facts were communicated to 
Dr. Sears, and in a letter dated May 11, 1880, he wrote that he 
regarded the matter of removal as settled, and the Normal 
College was established at Nashville permanently. 
The first appropriation of the. Peabody Board toward the 
running expenses of the College was in 1875, when $6,000.00 was 
contributed. This amount was increased to $9,000.00 the second 
year, and remained at about this figure until 1890, when more 
‘than $12,000.00 was given. This same year $12,000.00 was 
expended by the Board in the erection of Winthrop Schoo] on 
the campus, and the appropriation for library purposes was. 
co: mmenced. 
From 1890 to 1904, the annual appropriation by the Board 
for current expenses was between $13,000.00 and $15,000.00. 
In 1905 the amount was raised to $40,000.00, and again in 1907 
to $45,000.00, at which figure it has remained. In explanation 
of this large increase in recent years, it may be said that the 
annual State appropriation to the College ceased in 1905, for 
reasons which will hereafter be given. 
The State of Tennessee had been expected to give financial © 
assistance to the College from the very beginning, but for rea- 
sons previously referred to no appropriation was made until 
the legislative session of 1881, when $10,000.00 for each of two 
years was set aside.. In 1883 the State appropriation was 
” raised to $13,300.00, and was continued at this figure until 1891, 
except for the two years, 1887—’89, when only $11,500.00 ‘for 
each year was given. The legislature of 1891 appropriated 
$18,300.0, as did also its successor in 1893. In 1895, the annual 
appropriation was made $23,300.00, and remained at this figure 
until, 1905, when an annual donation was deemed no longer 
necessary, because of the $250,000.00 set aside by the State to 
be paid in ten annual installments to meet the conditions of the 
- Peabody Board for adequately endowing the College. This 
appropriation not being all immediately available, was not 2 
   
-P-A-G-E-390
FINANCIAL HISTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 878 
regarded by the Peabody Board as fulfilling conditions, and the 
endowment was not consummated. The legislature having 
already adjourned with no provision for the current expenses - 
of the College, the Peabody Board generously responded with 
an appropriation sufficient to meet all expenses and has con- 
tinued to do so. Since 1905 practically all the expenses of the 
College have been borne by the Peabody Trustees, they having 
voted so to do, until the matter of endowment was definitely 
settled. 
Peabody College has not only been fostered and financially 
cared for by the two agencies above referred to, but the Trustees 
of the University of Nashville have also been generous donors. 
It was their contribution of grounds and buildings and a small 
annuity at the first which made the establishment of the insti- 
tution at Nashville a possibility. And later, when it appeared 
that its removal to a neighboring State was inevitable, the 
Trustees made more liberal terms, and through them was 
secured a subscription of necessary funds and a guarantee of 
legislative assistance. 
Through all these years the College has used the campus and 
buildings of the University, appraised at about $250,000.00. 
At a rental value of five per cent, this would be equivalent to an 
annuity of $12,500.00, an aggregate for the thirty-four years of 
$425,000.00. The Trustees have spent some money for buildings 
on the campus—$10,000.00 for the Chancellor’s residence, 
$6,000.00 for the stone fence, $4,500.00 for the present office 
building, and several thousands in part payment for the Win- 
throp School. They have made repairs on the buildings from 
time to time, and have kept up the insurance on the same. The 
salary of the Chair of Modern Languages has also, in whole or 
in part, been paid out of funds in their possession. So that, 
all in all, besides the use of their grounds and buildings, they 
have contributed to the College, according to figuies in the 
report of President James D. Porter to the Peabody Board, 
October 8, 1903, more than $100,000.00. 
In making the record of the income of Peabody College, 
account must also be taken of the fees paid by the students. 
At no time in the history of the College have the charges been 
large, and the total amount received therefrom has constituted 
a very small percentage of the income of the College.
-P-A-G-E-391
B74 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
The first year of the College, the tuition rate was stated to 
be $45.00; but the fees received, according to our best estimate, 
amounted to only about $600.00. The second year only $3.00 was 
charged, and the entire fees were $273. From 1877~90, $6.00 
was charged for each student, the total being about $14,000.00. 
The fee was raised in 1890 to $10.00, and continued at this figure 
until 1901, during which time approximately $60,000 was 
received. For one year, 190102, a fee of $11.00 was charged, 
producing about $6,300.00. For the next two years it was 
raised to $12.00, and amounted to about $12,000.00. In 190408 
the fee was $15.00; the total received during the period being 
about $20,000.00. Last year, 190809, $30.00 was charged Col- 
lege students, and $15.00 those in the Normal School course, 
the amount received being about $6,000.00. In the first Summer 
School, 1902, no fee was charged. In the Summer Schools from 
1903 to 1908, there was a fee of $5.00, the total receipts being 
about $8,000.00. 
The grand total of all fees from students during the history 
of the College is less than $130,000.00, a very small amount in 
. comparison with what it has cost to run the institution. A 
detailed record of fees will be found in the table at the close of 
this article. 
No sketch of the financial history of Peabody College would 
be complete without some account of the scholarships estab- 
lished in 1877~’78 by the Peabody Board and continued to 
October 1, 1904. The number of beneficiaries the first year was 
nineteen, each receiving $200.00. Of these nineteen, ten were 
from Georgia, seven from Virginia, and two from Florida. By 
1880 the number had grown io eighty-three, calling for an 
annual appropriation of more than $16,000.00. 
For several years no scholarships were allotted to Tennessee, 
and of the first $10,000.00 appropriated in 1881 by the legislature 
to the College, $2,500.00 was set aside for the maintenance of 
scholarships for Tennessee students. Subsequently the State 
authorities made application to the Peabody Board to grant 
scholarships to Tennessee students on the same terms as to 
other States, which request was complied with in 1883. 
The scholarships allotted to Mississippi were withdrawn in 
1884, and from Florida the following year, because these States 
had failed to meet obligations on their bonds which were owned
-P-A-G-E-392
FINANCIAL HISTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 375 
by the Peabody Board. In 1892 their scholarships were renewed 
and continued to be enjoyed as in other States. 
The annuities paid to scholarship students continued at 
$200.00 until 1890, when it was voted to change to $100.00, with 
traveling expenses. This arrangement went into effect in 1893. 
The amount appropriated was not reduced, so that thereafter 
there were 174 scholarships instead of 114, as formerly. This 
arrangement remained in force until 1904, when in accordance 
with a resolution of the Peabody Board of the previous year, 
scholarships were no longer granted. 
It may be remarked in passing that the system of Peabody 
Scholarships was of great benefit to the Southern States. 
The scholarships were eagerly sought. In every State they were 
‘competed for. And because of them, hundreds of fine young 
men and women were enabled to prepare for teaching, who 
otherwise could not have done so; and the efficient service which 
they rendered in their respective States spread the benefits of 
Peabody College and the Peabody Board as could have been 
done in no other way. 
The teaching profession is more lucrative than it was ten or 
fifteen years ago, and the outlook for the teacher in the future 
is bright with promise; but for a number of years to come, no 
wiser expenditure of money could be made than to provide for 
the expenses of young men and women who will equip them- 
selves for teaching. 
The Alumni of Peabody College have already subscribed 
$10,000.00 as a Loan Fund to worthy students, about $1,500.00 
of which has been paidin. It is hoped that men and women of 
means throughout the South can be induced to swell this amount 
to. generous proportions sufficient to establish an adequate 
system of scholarships and fellowships in the George Peabody 
College for Teachers, and thus vastly increase its usefulness, not 
merely to men and women who will come to enjoy its benefits, 
but to every community in the South in which its graduates 
will do their work. 
Summarizing the financial contributions which the College 
has received during the years of its existence, we find that from 
first to last the Peabody Board has given for ordinary expenses, 
approximately $700,000.00, and for scholarships $570,000.00, a 
total of more than $1,250,000.00. At the same time the State
-P-A-G-E-393
376 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
has given $429,000:00; the University of Nashville, a home 
valued at about $250,000:00, and appropriations from time to 
time, aggregating more than $100,000.00; and from fees has 
been received about $180,000.00. A detailed statement of the 
contributions from all sources is appended to this article. . 
With the record of thé fostering care of the Peabody Trustees 
‘as outlined above, it is not to be wondered at that they have 
arranged for the adequate endowment of the College as a fitting 
monument to the noble soul who committed to them their trust. 
The intimate relationship between the College and the Pea- 
body Trustees is not a recent matter. They virtually originated 
the institution. Mr. Winthrop, in an address to the Board in 
1887, made this assertion. And Messrs. Choate and Olney, in 
February, 1907, in an elaborate report to the Board on the rela- 
tions of the Board to the College, repeat the same. 
The Peabody Trustees gave the first funds for current ex- 
penses; they’ induced the Tennessee authorities to assist in its 
maintenance; they continued, through all the years, to con- 
tribute liberally toward its support, and when other resources 
failed, they took upon themselves the burden of entirely pro- 
viding for it. . 
And they have always regarded the institution as theirs. 
As far back as 1887, when a successor to Dr. Stearns had to be 
chosen, they looked with jealous coneern at the disposition in 
some quarters to anticipate and forestall their action. Mr. 
Winthrop referred feelingly to this, and in the same connection, 
spoke of “our great Normal College at Nashville.” 
In the report of Messrs. Choate and Olney, above referred to, 
they write: “Though technically one department of the Univer- 
sity of Nashville, the organization and administration of the 
College have always been in charge of the Board, which prac- 
tically has conducted the affairs of the College as if it were an 
independent institution.” Statements of the same tenor from 
addresses. and reports could be multiplied, but this is sufficient 
to show the intimate relationship of the Peabody Trustees to 
the College, and to explain their attitude toward it. As be- 
fore stated, it was only natural, when the time came for wind- 
ing up their trust, that they should appropriate a generous 
portion of their funds to the College. 
To show how this was done, and to set forth the present
-P-A-G-E-394
FINANOIAL HISTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 377 
financial status of the College, is the purpose of the concluding 
lines of this paper. 
At the meeting of the Peabody Board in January, 1903, it 
was voted that the fund in its possession, or a portion thereof, 
should be applied to the establishment and maintenance of a 
Teachers’ College, to be called the “George Peabody College for 
Teachers,” at such a point in the Southern States as may be 
found desirable, and a committee was appointed to look into the 
matter and report at a subsequent meeting. The Board met in | 
October of the same year, but no report was made by the com- 
mittee at that time. It may be remembered by Peabody Alumni 
that on November 21, 1903, a number of representative Peabody 
men from various Southern States was called into a conference 
at Nashville. As a result of this conference, an organization 
was perfected, and later a large number of petitions in behalf 
of the College was sent to the Board from the most prominent 
educators and citizens of the South. At a subsequent meeting, 
in January, 1904, two Peabody Alumni, Messrs. J. R. Mosley, of 
Georgia, and 8. H. Bowman, of West Virginia, appeared before 
a committee of the Peabody Board to represent the interests of 
the College. It was voted at this meeting to continue the com- 
mittee for considering the question of establishing and main- 
taining at Nashville or elsewhere the George Peabody College 
for Teachers. . 
A year later, January 24, 1905, the Board voted that the 
time had arrived for closing the trust committed to it by Mr. 
Peabody and distributing the capital in its possession. It was 
also voted to apply one million dollars to the establishment of 
the George Peabody College for Teachers at Nashville, on the 
following conditions: : 
The County of Davidson, Tennessee, was to give $50,000.00; 
the city of Nashville, $200,000.00; the State of Tennessee, $250,- 
000.00; the Trustees of the University of Nashville, the grounds 
and buildings at present occupied by the College ; and $50,000.00 
was to be secured in addition. Subsequently it was agreed that 
the last $50,000.00 should come from Davidson County, making 
the appropriation from that source $100,000.00. 
It is hardly necessary to trace the history of the appropria- 
tions. Suffice it to say that Hon. James D. Porter, from the 
very beginning a consistent friend of the College, and during
-P-A-G-E-395
878 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY OOLLEHGE. 
these later years its efficient President, managed the whole 
affair with consummate skill. The trust reposed in him by all 
classes of citizens made it an easy matter and, one after another, 
the appropriations were secured. 
The County Court of Davidson voted on the proposition 
eight or ten times. Never was there an unfavorable vote, but 
delay in securing final action and change in the verbiage of 
resolutions necessitated these repetitions. 
The appropriation by the city of Nashville was secured by 
a direct yote of the people. An alumnus of the College, Mr. 
A. B. Anderson, then a member of the City Council, prepared 
the bill authorizing the submission of a bond issue to the people. 
In the election, on November 8, 1904, the proposition carried by 
approximately five to one. 
The State appropriation was made three times by successive 
legislatures. The first bill, passed in 1905, called for the pay- 
ment of $250,000.00 in ten annual installments. As already 
explained, the Peabody Board failed to recognize this as a literal 
compliance with conditions. Two years later, in 1907, a second 
bill was passed, appropriating the $250,000.00. The lawyers 
of the Peabody Board detected an error in this bill—an incon- 
sistent date—and refused to accept, because of the possibility 
of legal complications. Finally, in 1909, the appropriation was 
again made, this time to the satisfaction of all parties; and the 
Peabody Board, at its meeting, March, 1909, voted, “That Gov- 
ernor Porter notify the several donors of lands, bonds and 
money in aid of a corporation to be formed and organized under 
the name of the George Peabody College for Teachers, that the 
Trustees are ready to receive the donations in question, and, 
upon receiving the same, will at once proceed to form and organ- 
ize said corporation.” 
At this meeting also a committee of six was appointed to 
report a form of organization, suggest Trustees for the new 
corporation, and a program whereby the Board could create the 
College. In June of the same year the Peabody Board again 
met and appointed Trustees, to whom certain instructions as 
to the establishment of the College were given in a detailed 
letter from the Committee of the Peabody Board having the 
matter in hand. 
At last it seemed to the friends of the College that the George
-P-A-G-E-396
FINANOIAL HISTORY OF PEABODY OOLLHEGH. 379 
Peabody College for Teachers was an assured fact, and interest 
was centered on the meeting of the Trustees to be held in Nash- 
ville, October 5, 1909. But the day prior to their meeting, the 
County Court of Davidson County, moved by rumors of an 
alliance with Vanderbilt University, took steps to withhold 
its bonds, which had months before been prepared and handed 
over to Governor Porter for the Peabody College. The Peabody 
Board, thereupon, on October 8th, in annual session, again 
voted to delay final action, pending the action of the County 
Court of Davidson County at.the meeting in January, 1910. 
At the January, 1910, session of the County Court, after 
much discussion and parliamentary wrangling, it was voted to 
turn over the $100,000.00 of county bonds for the new College. 
The question of the constitutionality of the bond issue was then 
raised; and at this time it is impossible to state with certainty 
what the financial status of the College is. 
If the matter is settled the institution will be upon a sub- 
stantial basis financially. It will-have at its disposal $1,000,- 
000.00 donated by the Peabody Board; grounds and buildings, 
valued at $250,000.00, by the University of Nashville Trustees; 
$250,000.00, by the State of Tennessee; $200,000.00, by the city 
of Nashville; and $100,000.00, by the County of Davidson. 
The table appended gives a detailed record of the revenue of 
the College. TEixact dollars and cents have not been given, but 
the round numbers are fairly accurate. 
The appropriations from the Peabody Fund have been com- 
piled from reports of the General Agents of the Board, made at 
annual meetings. In one year only—the year of the death of 
Barnas Sears—is there uncertainty as to the amount, and for 
that year an estimate is made, based on the contributions of the 
years immediately preceding and following. 
The gifts of the State have been compiled from the biennial 
reports of the State Comptroller. 
The contributions of the University of Nashville have been 
estimated from records in the office of the College, and there has 
been added a conservative rental value of the campus and build- 
ings which have been used by the College. It has been impos- 
sible to obtain exact data of the University donations, but it 
appears that for a number of years practically the entire inter- 
est on the bonds belonging to the University were devoted to the -
-P-A-G-E-397
380 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PHABODY COLLEGE, 
: College. Special donations were made for the Chancellor’s 
‘residence, 188081; Winthrop School, 189091; Office, 1892- 
93; and Fence, 1900-01. In later years a considerable part of 
the funds of the University have been used for the Medical . 
Department, which explains the decreasing amounts for the 
College. . 
-It should be stated that the table does not include the sal- 
aries of the various Presidents of the institution. They have 
apparently been regarded as officers of the Peabody Board, and 
their salaries have been paid directly to them and not through 
the College. The total amount for this purpose approximates 
$150,000.00. 
All in all, it is believed that this statement furnishes a rea- 
sorably correct record of the income of the College for the thirty- 
four years of its existence.
