THE INFLUENCE OF PEABODY COLLEGE 
FOR TEACHERS UPON SOUTH- 
ERN EDUCATION. 
By Dr. CHaries E. Lirria, Prormssor or Lavin. 
  
I. Tux work of the Peabody Board and the Peabody College 
for Teachers has been so much a unity that the spirit and the 
effort of the one can be considered the spirit and effort of the 
other. In the same way, the results found to be due from the 
College can be gratefully attributed to the Board. This inti- 
mate association comes from the fact that the two are almost 
contemporary in period of operation and nearly coéxtensive in 
the territory reached. Besides, the Board has centered upon 
the College nearly one-half of its entire income, and has spent 
upon this one institution fully $1,250,000 in thirty-four years 
for reaching the schools of the South, while hardly more than 
this has been spent in forty-two years upon more than a dozen 
other beneficiaries. The first year’s work of the Board was a 
thorough effort to map out a sane policy for the future, and 
resulted in three meetings for a total of six days, thus starting 
a noble enterprise in a notable manner. In these meetings, the 
first general Agent of the Board, Dr. Barnas Sears, brought 
fully to consciousness the lines of work they were to prosecute. 
They determined to promote the establishment of primary public 
school education and of normal school education for teachers. 
Dr. Sears was in constant consultation with Mr. Peabody and 
Mr. Winthrop during the formative years, 1867-69, and conse- 
quently the policy of the Board was largely determined under 
the very eye of Mr. Peabody himself. “He lived for over two 
- years after the organization of the Board, had frequent’ con- 
ferences with Mr. Winthrop in 1867, 1868, 1869, and notably 
with Dr. Sears during August and September of 1869, when 
they were together at Virginia Springs. 
(383)
-P-A-G-E-401
884 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 
Mr. Peabody was present at the meeting in New York in 
1867, present again at the seventh meeting held at Newport 
in July, 1869, just afew months before his death. Though 
in ill health, he came into the meeting of the Board for the 
closing prayer by Bishop McIlvaine. This was his last official 
connection with the great educational philanthropy he had 
founded. . 
These three men are the founders of the work of the Pea- 
. body Board, as they are the founders of Peabody College. The 
true spirit of philanthropy illustrated by George Peabody 
made a profound impression upon the other two, and the 
words of Mr. Winthrop in his eulogy at the tomb of Peabody 
are not merely a noble tribute of affection, but they have 
continued to be a trumpet call: “And-so we bid thee fare- 
well, brave, honest, noble-hearted Friend of Mankind.” No 
fitter tribute can be paid to the gentle spirit of George Pea- 
body in words, but perhaps even a nobler tribute has been 
paid to him by the results wrought for education in the South 
by the Board and the College. The Board has always stood 
for what has been. finest and highest, and its approach has 
always been hailed with perfect sincerity and heartiness 
wherever it has offered to assist or to direct. 
The College has shared in this generous regard bestowed by 
the Southern people and has found itself constantly appealed 
to as a guide, because of the great esteem with which the 
Board has been regarded. 
Few institutions can boast such founders as the three 
here specially named, any one of whom would be a rich heritage 
to claim. : 
- II. The first aim of the Peabody Board was to help the 
prostrate governments in the states to bring some order out 
of their educational chaos. Upon this they worked specifi- 
cally from 1867 to 1875. So far had the Southern states re- 
covered from political and educational confusion in the pe- 
riod from 1875 to 1880, that Dr. Sears in his report for 1879 
could write as follows: “Of the two grand objects which 
this Board has from the beginning had in view, namely, the 
promotion of common school education and the professional 
training of teachers, the former or primary one has been so _ 
far attained that it may, in. great part, be safely left in the
-P-A-G-E-402
INFLUENCE OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 385 
hands of the people, and our chief attention henceforth be 
given to the latter.” 
The establishment of the College fell, therefore, in a sig- 
nificant era, both from the standpoint of educational condi- 
tions in the South, and of the plan of the Board. From its 
‘beginning, in 1875, its object was to assist the entire South 
in its strenuous efforts to make progress with its system of 
public education. Dr. Sears saw perhaps more clearly than 
anybody, that “no system of public instruction is complete 
which does not embrace professional schools.” He refers in 
his report of 1875 with some pride to the establishment of 
“a great Normal School at Nashville for the State of Tennes- 
see.” Unfortunately for the College, Dr. Sears died before 
five years of its work had been completed. And thus, as the 
remaining executor of Mr. Peabody’s wishes, Mr. Winthrop 
was left to continue the work into still further phases. But 
Dr. Sears had already begun the extension of the College to 
all the South by the system of scholarships established in 1877. 
Mr. Winthrop had. seen public schools promoted and per- 
manent systems established; he had seen schools of all grades 
helped and encouraged in numerous places; he had seen normal 
schools for the training of teachers take shape in two or three; 
he had seen the school at Nashville established with more 
resources and a higher ambition than all the rest; and before 
his death he finally made the memorable suggestion, that when 
the fund should be distributed Peabody College should receive 
at least $1,000,000 and be made the lasting monument of George 
Peabody. His suggestion bore fruit some jen years after it 
was uttered. : : 
III. Meanwhile the College in its growth was not only send- 
ing out active workers into all the South, but was becom- 
ing the direct inspiration and parent of normal schools in 
the states. 
When the College was opened, in 1875, not a normal school 
of any importance was to be found in the South. A few private 
schools might have been so called by courtesy, but the states 
had not yet begun the business of training their teachers. 
True, the State Normal School at Fairmont, W. Va., had its 
beginning in 1867, though its vigorous life from and for the 
state dates from 1892; and also the State Normal School at 
25
-P-A-G-E-403
386 ALUMNI DIRECTORY OF PHABODY COLLEGE. 
Florence, Ala., was founded in 1872, but its first impulse into 
state-wide service came in 1887. A similar history is found 
for the schools at Glenville and at Huntington, W. Va. 
Peabody College has been interested primarily in public 
normal schools, not private ones. Its effort has always been 
to promote the establishment of such state-supported schools. 
And to its influence, direct and indirect, has been largely due 
the wonderful growth of the public normal school in the South 
during the period of that influence. A glance at this growth 
will be worth while. Incidentally, it may be favorably noted 
that a general tendency of increase in the public normal schools 
and of decrease in the private normal schools, both as to number 
and equipment, is a marked feature of recent educational his- 
tory; see the comments of the United States Commissioner of 
Education, Report 1908, vol. 2, pp. 817-819. 
Between 1875 and 1880 only one normal school was estab- 
lished in the Peabody territory by ‘a state—the one at Hunts- 
ville, Texas (coéducational). Between 1880 and 1890 six were 
established by the states: at Farmville (for females) and at 
Williamsburg (for males), Va.; at Florence and at Troy, Ala. 
(coéducational) ; at De Funiak Springs, Fla. (coéducational ; 
now merged with the University of Florida, Gainesville) ; at 
Natchitoches, La. (coéducational). Between 1890 and 1900 
the states were even more liberal and accepted in good earnest 
the responsibility of training their elementary teachers. Dur- 
ing this period twelve normal schools were established: at 
Athens, at Fairmont, at Glenville, at Huntington, at Shepherds- 
town, and at West Liberty, W. Va. (coéducational) ; at Greens- 
boro, N. C. (for females) ; at Rock Hill, 8. C. (for females) ; at 
Athens (coéducational) and at Milledgeville (for females), Ga.; 
at Livingston (for females) and at Jacksonville (coéduca- 
tional), Ala. From 1900 to 1909 there have been established 
eight normal schools: at Harrisonburg, Va. (for females); at 
Bowling Green and at Richmond, Ky. (coéducational); at 
Denton and at San Marcos, Tex, (coéducational) ; at Alva, at 
Edmond, and at Weatherford, Okla. (coéducational). With 
these twenty-six normal schools already in operation, eight 
others have been recently provided for by the state legislatures 
and are in process of equipping for the reception of pupils: 
another in Virginia at Fredericksburg, another in North Caro-
-P-A-G-E-404
INFLUENCE OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 387 
* lina, two others in Alabama, three in Tennessee, and one in 
Arkansas. : 
Most of these schools are doing valiant service in their 
states for the cause of professionally trained teachers. They 
are attacking with vigor and specifically the question of training 
elementary teachers, of equipping the great army “behind the 
guns” in our educational advance. The schools for women alone 
are also making a real contribution to home-making, by offering 
many courses which combine in a fruitful way the double value 
of culture and of practical domestic economy. <A few are 
vaguely ambitious to be called colleges and are getting: away 
from closely gripping their real problems; but the number in 
this restless, fluctuating condition is smaller each year. The 
majority are finding their proper function, are serving their 
states well. They are yielding to the sane demands for “spheres 
of influence” and for the standardization of all our schools into 
types, so as to combine the good qualities of a series of educa- 
tional agencies, rigid in their standards and definite in their 
aims, but flexible in their direction and their gifts of service. 
The facts of our life and the conditions under which we find 
society working must determine for us the nature and the aim 
of our schools, each community or group to satisfy its own . 
needs and to correlate effort with surrounding groups. These 
tests are being efficiently met in almost all our normal schools. 
An idea of the power these schools are now exerting on the 
state systems can be gained to some extent by a statement cover- 
ing their equipment, support, and enrollment. The tignres to 
follow are based upon a comparison of recent catalogues of the 
schools and statistics of the United States Commissioner of 
Education, Report 1908, vol. 2, pp. 888-851. They are only 
approximately correct, but are exact enough not to vitiate any 
conclusions drawn from them. This table summarizes the 
present status of normal school education in the South:

Soon to be added to these are the eight new schools being 
established in Virginia, North Carolina, Alabama, Tennessee, 
and Arkansas, which are all liberally provided for and will open 
with bright prospects. That these twenty-six schools, in the 
ten states named in the table above, have plants totaling 
$3,991,000, annual appropriations aggregating $751,000, and a 
grand total of 12,035 pupils (presumably not below normal and 
high-school grades), is surely a magnificent accomplishment for 
the era of normal school growth in the Peabody territory: 
Henceforth the states can be counted upon to care adequately 
for the professional training of their elementary teachers. 
For the first twenty-five years of the College its influ- 
ence was direct and unmistakable upon the task each state 
began to assume in training its own elementary teachers. This 
influence is proved not merely by the dates and circumstances 
attending the establishment of these normal schools, but by 
the fact that throughout their history they have frequently 
been headed by a Peabody graduate and have at all times 
had a Jarge number of such graduates in their teaching force. 
It can be proved still further by a comparison of the type of 
work and aim of the schools as set forth in their early pub- 
lications and as compared with those of Peabody. 
. IV. While an educational awakening world-wide in extent 
has been going on for some fifteen years, nowhere has it been 
so striking as in the South during the last ten. It may not 
be fair to claim the largest share of this awakening as a re- 
sult of the work of Peabody College, or of the College and
-P-A-G-E-406
INFLUENCE OF PEABODY COLLEGE. 389 
Board combined. But certainly no one factor contributed as 
much to this end as the steady stream of graduates turned out 
from the College at Nashville and distributed to all points of the 
South, a body of permanent recruits to the educational army. 
Peabody graduates went forth in large numbers each year, 
they were true to their mission in behalf of public education, 
they became permanent members of the teaching profession. 
In this way the steady, wide-spread, persistent work for edu- 
cation from 1875 to 1900 prepared a soil ready for the cheer- 
ful reception of educational seed. The enthusiasm, the buoy- 
ancy, and the optimistic spirit shown in our educational re- 
vival since that time can be largely accounted for when we 
think of the facts mentioned. And some of the sanity and 
constructive wisdom shown in these generous movements are 
traceable to the same source. . : 
Vv. A compilation in the present volume gives a list by 
classes and years of all the graduates of the College. A digest 
of these names has been made to show the present contribu- 
tion that Peabody College is making to the educational forces 
at work in each state. Exact comparative statistics cannot be 
made, but figures furnished in December by the Department of 
Education of the State of Alabama for high schools alone will 
probably show something of the ratio in other states and other 
types of schools. In the 90 high schools of Alabama the total 
number of teachers is 345, with 106 holding less than normal 
school diplomas. Of the remaining 239, 174 hold college degrees 
and 65 are normal school graduates. The ten Alabama institu- 
tions furnish 128 out of the 239, while Peabody College fur- 
nishes 19. This is 8 per cent of all holding degrees and di- 
plomas, while compared with the 65 who are professionally 
trained it amounts to 29 per cent. In addition to the figures 
below, which are based upon an actual count of the names in 
this Directory, it would be fair to estimate a proportionate 
number engaged in teaching who are ex-students of the College, 
non-graduates. No exact figures have been gathered for this 
large class, who add in their quiet way an immense power to the 
volume of Peabody influence. In connection, therefore, with 
the figures given for each state, a very conservative estimate is 
made of this contingent. Here are the statistics in tabular 
form:

Literature, The Keiogijuku, Tokio, Japan; Macon A. Leiper, 
Professor of Latin, State Normal School, Bowling Green, Ky. 
VI. With the growth of the College as a Normal School, 
with the aspiration urged upon it by Mr. Winthrop, with the 
enlarged opportunities that came about ten years ago, with 
the new demands plainly seen in the readjustment of edu- 
cational forces, the College saw clearly its needs and its op- 
portunity. It saw that expansion and permanency were its 
needs, and that stress should thenceforth be laid on the Col- 
lege type of its work and that its Normal School work should 
be turned over to the states. It saw as its opportunity, the 
power to act as an educational clearing house for all the 
South, to train efficient leaders for every department of work, 
and skillful experimenters for every new idea. It saw, too, 
that unnecessary confusion leads to duplication of work, and 
that clearness of idea makes possible an interrelation of schools, 
whereby due regard can be paid to the several levels or strata 
of school work (primary, elementary, high school, college, uni- 
versity), and at the same time allow all possible latitude for 
the numerous lines upward through these strata (liberal, nor- 
mal, technical, medical, theological, industrial, for defectives, 
etc., ete.). In accordance with this view, the College endorsed 
from the beginning the movement for college standards, as 
promulgated by the Association of Colleges and Prepara- 
tory Schools of the Southern States, founded in 1895. While 
not in line for membership in the Association, it none 
the less believed in its principles, and in 1903, when 
opportunity allowed, raised its standard to college rank in 
accordance with the standard of that Association. When 
the Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching 
began in 1906 its campaign for a national standard and 
a national awakening on the subject of standards, Peabody 
College again gave its adherence to the principles formulated 
and in 1908 raised her standard accordingly. This was done 
in spite of opposition from many sources, and in spite of a 
concerted move on the part of some Southern colleges to pe- 
tition the Carnegie Foundation not to set so high a standard, 
arguing that Southern conditions would not allow Southern
-P-A-G-E-412
INFLUENOE OF PHABODY COLLEGE. 395 
colleges to meet it. The College took the view that the South 
should be ready to accept the fact of its consolidation, but 
should resent isolation; should accept its provincialism so far 
as indicative of individuality, but resent backwardness; 
should be ready to make progress along ali the constructive 
lines, so wonderfully begun in the last few years. These views 
the College set forth emphatically and clearly in a bulletin is- 
sued in September, 1908. ~ 
From these facts it seems plain that the influence of the 
College upon Southern education of every grade has been pro- 
nounced and constant, that Peabody men and women are a 
powerful force in the elementary and high schools of the 
state systems, in the colleges, in the normal schools, and in ad- 
ministrative positions. Perhaps no other institution on the 
continent can show such a large number of graduates engaged 
_in all phases of education, nor such professional solidarity. 
The College has been true to its mission and its aim, and 
has justified by its past work and its present influence a per- 
- petuity—an enlarged and dominant perpetuity.

THE ROBERT WINTHROP SCHOOL. 
  
Tur Robert Winthrop School of Peabody College was estab- 
lished two years ago. It is the successor to what was once 
known as the Winthrop Model School, which was established 
originally as a primary school of observation for students taking 
courses of primary education in Peabody College. This 
Model School at first (1889) included only the four primary 
grades. Later (1892) the number of grades was increased to 
eight, and still later (1894) to eleven grades, in order to accom- 
modate students of the College who were conditioned for 
entrance and to enlarge the scope of observation. 
In 1907 the College authorities decided to provide for and 
require practice teaching as well as observation work of all 
students taking professional studies in Peabody College. They 
also decided that both the practice and observation work should 
cover not simply the elementary grades, but should extend also 
through four years of secondary studies, thus covering the four- 
teen units demanded by the Carnegie entrance requirements. 
This decision necessitated a complete reorganization of the 
Model School.. It involved a decided change in its course of 
study, both as to content and extent, increased equipment, and 
larger teaching force. 
The first step in this reorganization was taken when arrange- 
ments were made with the Board of Education of the city of 
Nashville whereby the students in the Department of Education 
of Peabody College should, under the supervision of competent 
critic teachers, teach and observe, fifty hours each year, in the 
elementary grades of the public schools of the city. This trans- 
ference of elementary practice and observation to the city 
schools eliminated the necessity of longer maintaining -an 
elementary school on the Peabody campus; the elementary 
department of the Model School was, therefore, discontinued. 
To provide for practice and observation in a secondary 
‘school, the present Robert Winthrop School was organized. 
Its course of study includes four years in English, mathematics, 
(897)
-P-A-G-E-415
888 ALUMNI DIRHOTORY OF PHABODY COLLEGE. 
Latin, Greek, and history; and two or more years in German, 
French, science, music, art and physical training. Ali students 
of the College who are taking educational courses that lead to 
the degree of Bachelor of Arts are now required to do practice 
work, and observe methods in this school. : 
The prime function of the Robert Winthrop School, there- 
fore, is to furnish a practice and observation field for students 
of Peabody College who are candidates for the Bachelor’s 
’ degree. . 
A second function of this school is to afford opportunity for 
students able to enter the College except in case of one or two 
subjects to make up these deficiencies while still pursuing the 
regular college work. 
This school has also become a strong feeder to Peabody 
College by sending a large proportion of its graduates to 
matriculate as students of the College. 
Any young man or woman of good character is eligible to 
membership in this school if he or she is prepared to enter a 
first-class high school. Students who complete the course of 
study at Robert Winthrop are thoroughly equipped to meet the 
A. B. entrance requirements of the best Universities in the 
Jand. 
Both last year and this year the seating-capacity limit of the 
school was reached by those applying for entrance on the first 
day. Many have been turned away each year because of lack of 
room. The enroliment this year (209) comes largely from 
Nashville and surrounding towns, though many States are rep- 
resented by pupils now in attendance. 
A spirit of enthusiasm and earnest devotion to work per- 
vades every department of the school. There are two boys’ 
debating societies, two girls’ literary societies, two glee clubs, 
and regular class organizations besides athietic teams in basket- 
ball, football, baseball, tennis, and track-meet contests. 
Every member of the teaching force is a college graduate, 
has had thorough training in both the subject-matter to be 
taught and the fundamental principles of method which underlie 
all efficient teaching. Each member of the faculty has been 
selected because of special equipment for the work assigned.
